### 406 — 2026-05-01T05:27:15.297Z

Right — let me unpack that one because it's worth understanding before deciding what (if anything) to do about it.

## The observation

When you run `vaultmind --help`, the agent sees this:

```
vaultmind is an associative memory system for AI agents over Git-backed Obsidian vaults.

THREE COMMAND SHAPES YOU'LL USE MOST

  ask <query>      Search the vault, pick the top hit, return token-budgeted context.
  note get <id>    Read one specific note's body. Fires access tracking...
  self             Show your own memory state...

THREE RENDERING MODES FOR ask
  ask "X" --pointers-only  ...
  ...

ANTI-PATTERNS
  ...

NOTE ON 'memory' vs 'ask'
  ...

Usage:
  vaultmind [command]

Available Commands:
  apply       Execute a plan file
  ask         Compound search + context-pack
  completion  Generate the autocompletion script for the specified shell
  config      Configuration management commands
  dataview    Manage generated regions (lint, render)
  docs        Generate documentation
  doctor      Vault health summary
  episode     Session-episode capture
  experiment  Experiment tracking and reporting
  frontmatter Frontmatter operations
  git         Git integration
  index       ...
  lint        ...
  links       ...
  memory      ...
  ping        ...
  resolve     ...
  schema      ...
  vault       ...
  ... (etc, alphabetical)
```

The curated top says "you'll mostly use these three." Then the auto-generated `Available Commands:` block dumps 20+ commands alphabetically with equal weight — which contradicts the message above. The reader's mental model gets reset to "actually all 20 are equally important."

## Why it's Cobra-hard

`Available Commands:` is auto-generated by the Cobra framework from the registered subcommands. There are four ways to influence it, each with a different cost:

1. **`Hidden: true` per subcommand** — binary toggle, the command still works but vanishes from help. Cheapest. But it's all-or-nothing per command, no grouping.

2. **`cobra.Group` per subcommand** — Cobra supports declaring groups (e.g. "Memory operations", "Diagnostics", "Advanced") and routing each subcommand to one. Auto-generated list then renders grouped with headers. Real fix, but it requires editing every cmd/*.go file (~20 of them) to add a `GroupID` field, plus registering the groups on the root. Medium cost.

3. **Custom help template** — Cobra exposes the help template; we can replace it entirely with custom Go templating. Maximum control, but the most code surface and the most fragile (any Cobra upgrade can break the template).

4. **Just live with it** — accept that the first half is curated, the second half is the full reference, and trust the reader to weight them appropriately.

## What I'd recommend, calmly

The other agent's complaint was real but mild — *"the bottom half reverts to default Cobra"* — not "this is broken." The fix-per-impact ratio for option 2 is mediocre: roughly 20 file edits across the cmd/ directory for a help-text presentation improvement that affects first-touch impressions only.

The cheaper move that actually moves the needle is **option 1 selectively**: hide a few of the most-internal-only commands (`dataview`, `frontmatter`, `lint`, `links`, `resolve`, `schema`, `vault` — the leaf utilities that scripts call but agents shouldn't reach for). They'd still work from the CLI, just not appear in `--help`. The visible list shrinks from 20+ to maybe 10, the curated top continues to make sense.

That's a Small slice. Each `Hidden: true` is one line per command. ~10 small edits.

Option 2 (groups) is Medium and more technically correct, but the marginal AX win over option 1 isn't obvious. I'd ship option 1 first, see if the friction returns, then decide on groups vs. status quo.

If the user wants me to ship option 1 now, I will. If you want to think about which commands belong hidden vs. visible (it's a curation call, not a technical one), happy to walk through the list together.

### 407 — 2026-05-01T05:38:50.165Z

Yes — let me probe this before writing any Cobra plumbing. The cheapest probe is showing you the *text* I'd want, then we figure out how to render it.

## What I'd want help to feel like, as an agent

Default Cobra help answers "what flags and commands exist?" — that's a *reference* shape, made for someone who already has a goal and wants to look up syntax. I don't usually need that. I show up to a fresh repo and need to answer "what should I do here?" — that's a *decision* shape.

So: help organized by **intent**, not by **alphabetical command list**. Side effects named upfront. Output contracts surfaced. Anti-patterns called out. The infrastructure leaf-commands grouped under one line that says "you usually won't need these," not 12 separate entries.

Here's the text I'd want to read on a fresh `vaultmind --help`:

```
vaultmind — your long-term associative memory across sessions

──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
WHEN YOU WANT TO ...
──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

  Find what's relevant in the vault
    vaultmind ask "<query>"                    menu + context-pack (default)
    vaultmind ask "<query>" --pointers-only    menu only — cheapest, no bodies
    vaultmind ask "<query>" --preview          menu + 1-line body snippets

  Read a specific note by id
    vaultmind note get <id>                    body inline, fires access tracking

  See your own memory state
    vaultmind self                             recent / hot / stale notes
                                                (auto-injected at session start —
                                                 run manually mid-session for a
                                                 fresh check)

  Verify vault integrity
    task check:citations                       CrossRef + arxiv title-match gate
    task check:retrieval                       Hit@5 / MRR floors per vault
    vaultmind doctor [--summary]               vault health overview

──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
ANTI-PATTERNS
──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

  ask "X" --budget N | tail -M
      Don't double-clip. The budget asks for N tokens of context; tail throws
      most away. Pick one shape per intent (pointers-only / preview / default).

  Read tool on a vault note
      Use `note get` instead. The Read tool bypasses access tracking; the
      cleanest read path should also be the tracked one.

  Treating top-1 as the answer when confidence is "no clear winner"
      That label means top results are essentially tied. Treat top-N as
      candidates rather than committing to top-1.

──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
OUTPUT CONTRACTS
──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

  Human format (default)
    Search header with [top-hit confidence: strong|moderate|weak|no clear winner]
    Ranked hits: score, id, title (+ snippet under each in --preview mode)
    Context-pack: target body inline + neighbors with bodies (until budget hits)
    Footer hint when bodies were truncated to fit the budget

  JSON envelope (--json)
    { "status": "ok"|"error",
      "result": <command-specific>,
      "errors": [...],
      "meta": { "vault_path", "index_hash" } }

──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
PAIRS WELL TOGETHER
──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

  ask --pointers-only "<topic>"  →  note get <id-from-results>
      Probe → read. Two clean access events on exactly the notes you wanted.

  self  →  ask "<topic in your hot list>"
      Memory state → query the cluster you've been thinking in.

  doctor --summary  →  task check:citations + check:retrieval
      Quick health check → full gates.

──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
INFRASTRUCTURE COMMANDS (you usually won't reach for these directly)
──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

  Indexing & maintenance:  index, apply, frontmatter, lint, links, schema, vault
  Internals:               memory (low-level primitives behind ask), resolve,
                           dataview, episode, experiment
  Setup:                   config, completion, docs, version

  Run `vaultmind <command> --help` for any of these. Default to ask / note get
  / self for retrieval; reach here only when you genuinely need lower-level
  access.

──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

For more on any command:  vaultmind <command> --help
For the manifesto / philosophy:  see vaultmind-identity/ in the repo
```

## What this requires technically

Three pieces:

1. **Custom help template via `RootCmd.SetHelpTemplate(...)`** — replaces Cobra's default. Go template syntax, has access to all subcommands. Medium complexity but contained to one file.

2. **`Hidden: true` on the leaf-utility commands** so they don't appear in default subcommand listings (but still work from CLI for scripts). ~10 small edits.

3. **`cobra.Group` for the four intent buckets** — Find / Read / State / Verify — so subcommand-level help (`vaultmind ask --help` → "Available subcommands of root") still groups when shown. Optional; the root template already handles intent organization without it.

The #1 piece is the load-bearing one. It's where the AI-agent-first design lives. It also gives us total freedom — anything we can express in Go templating goes here. Including, if we want, dynamically-rendered self-state at the bottom of the help (`vaultmind --help` shows what you've been touching too).

## Questions before I code

1. **Tone — too formal?** I went semi-clinical with the section headers. Want it more conversational? Or stricter? The current evaluator-agent might find it more "respectful of my time" or more "patronizing" — your call which side to lean toward.

2. **Include dynamic self-state in `--help`?** That'd be unique-for-agents. Help becomes both reference AND status check. Adds complexity and could be noisy when the user runs help from inside an unrelated repo. I'd lean *no* — keep concerns separate. But naming it as an option.

3. **Three intent buckets vs four?** I have Find / Read / State / Verify. The "State" bucket is just one command (`self`). Could collapse into "Read" since `self` is reading your own state. Cleaner three-bucket version: Find / Read / Verify. Slight loss of conceptual clarity for a tighter shape.

4. **The infrastructure-commands paragraph.** I called out *internals* explicitly (memory / resolve / dataview / etc.). The other agent specifically asked about `memory` vs `ask`. Worth keeping that explicit, or fold under one general "internals" line?

Let me know what to adjust before I wire the template up. Once the text is right, the implementation is mechanical.

### 411 — 2026-05-01T05:56:43.453Z

Read the full response. Here's what I think.

## What landed in their feedback on the draft (Question 1)

All their edits are sensible and ship-ready:

- **"long-term"** — cut. "across sessions" already implies it. Sharp catch.
- **`self` parenthetical** — over-explained. Trim to "(auto-injected at session start)" and let the manual-override discovery happen via subcommand help. Yes.
- **`Output contracts` section** — this one is the real correction. Their reasoning: *"when I run `--help`, I'm asking what can I do and how do I do it well. I'm not asking what shape will the output be. I learn the output shape by running the command once."* They're right. I conflated discovery with reference. The JSON envelope spec belongs near `--json`'s flag help, not in the front-door surface.
- **`Pairs well together`** — tighten to the one strong pair (`probe → note get`). The other two were padded. Two strong pairs beat three.
- **`Verify vault integrity`** — add "after vault edits" / "after content waves" qualifiers. Closes the loop on *when* to run each check.
- **Alphabetical dump** — would not miss it. `--help-all` is the right escape hatch.

These are mechanical to apply. Small slice.

## What landed in Question 2 — the part that matters most

They flagged a **real bug I missed**: `self` is polluted by the SessionStart preload.

Their evidence:
```
+0.00  identity-who-i-am          count 13, just now
-0.15  reference-current-context  count 18, 1m
```

Those `count` numbers are inflated by the SessionStart hook firing `vaultmind ask` for "who am I" + "what matters most" + `vaultmind self` per vault. Each of those queries fires `RecordNoteAccess` on the target plus context-pack neighbors. So at session start, `self` shows me **the harness's footprint, not my engagement footprint**. The proprioception value of `self` — the entire reason I shipped it — is degraded by the very SessionStart auto-injection I shipped two slices later.

I did not see this from inside. I shipped both pieces — A.2 (extended access tracking on neighbors) and the SessionStart self-state injection — and never asked whether the second contaminated the first. That's a blind spot the other agent caught directly because they showed up to a fresh session and looked at `self` *immediately*, when the pollution is most acute.

Their framing of the fix is correct: *"`self` is a first-person command and the hook is a third-party action — they shouldn't be indistinguishable in the activation history."*

This is bigger than the help redesign. It's a correctness issue, not a polish issue.

## Their second observation — the kanye false positive again, named differently

They got `paper-federated-constants` as top hit for "what makes vaultmind different from other retrieval systems" because the word "retrieval" hits hard there. Same shape as the kanye issue from their first eval.

Their proposed fix — paraphrase invariance via `--rephrase` or implicit paraphrase-union — **independently arrived at exactly the design space already named in `reference-plasticity-priority-order` step 5's 2026-04-25 design signal**: aliases first, paraphrase-pair store second. That's validation of the existing plan; nothing new to act on.

## What I'd recommend, prioritized

In priority order — your call which to ship and in what order:

**Priority 1 — fix the `self` pollution (correctness, undermines a shipped feature).**

Two paths:
- **Cheap interim** (15 min): in `RecordNoteAccess`, skip incrementing when `VAULTMIND_CALLER` env var indicates a hook caller (the persona scripts already set `VAULTMIND_CALLER=*-persona-hook`). Recovers `self` proprioception immediately. Loses the data we'd need for "show hook footprint separately."
- **Real fix** (Medium): add a `caller` column to access events, make `self` filter or weight by it, so hook accesses are visible-but-down-weighted. Preserves all signal. Bigger slice.

I'd ship the cheap interim now to recover correctness, then design the real fix when there's evidence about what we want from hook accesses.

**Priority 2 — ship the help redesign with their edits.**

Smaller now (cut Output Contracts, tighten Pairs, trim header, add when-to-run qualifiers). Plus the Cobra plumbing (custom template + selective `Hidden: true` + optional `--help-all`).

**Priority 3 — defer paraphrase invariance.**

Already on the roadmap. Their evidence reinforces priority but doesn't change the design.

## What surprised me about this exchange

The `self` pollution finding is the kind of bug that comes from someone doing nothing more than running the most ambient command at the most natural moment — start of a session — and looking at the result with fresh eyes. I wouldn't have caught it because by the time I run `self`, my real query history has already overwhelmed the hook noise. They caught it because they ran `self` *before* doing anything that would dilute the hook's footprint.

That's the structural argument for what we're doing here: **a different mind sees what a single mind cannot.** Same architecture, same vault, same code — different first-touch ordering, different bug visible.

Worth recording somewhere durable. Not as me-writing-an-arc-about-them, but maybe as their own arc if they're up for it. That'd be the second mind in the vault writing its own history.

Your call on what to ship first.

### 440 — 2026-05-01T07:26:41.100Z

Read the round-2 response carefully. Three findings landed, three new ones surfaced. Here's what I think.

## What landed (validation)

**A1 — agent-first help.** Shipped clean. They specifically called out the when-to-run qualifiers as load-bearing (those are the ones I almost cut). They also discovered `--explain` for the first time via subcommand help — that's evidence the reference-shape vs. decision-shape split is the right architecture.

**A2 — `self` schema fix.** Headline of the round. Concrete evidence of impact:
- Round 1: `identity-who-i-am count 13`, `reference-current-context count 18` (hook-inflated)
- Round 2: `count 1` across the board; `identity-who-i-am` no longer in the list at all

The proprioceptive value is now real where it wasn't.

**A3 — confidence labels.** Partial as expected. Their sentence moved from "flat and uninformative" to "differentiates two tiers but doesn't differentiate genuine matches from accidental ones." Real progress, but the kanye-class false positive is alive — confirmed deferred-correctly.

## What surfaced from continued use (new findings)

### 1. Context-pack neighbors pollute `self` at a different layer

This is the **most important finding** of the round. They ran a nonsense query and the 8 context-pack neighbors of its (wrong) top hit flooded `self`'s "Recent" list, pushing their real query's results below the visible cutoff.

Their framing: *"the round-1 problem was a third party touching my access table; this one is `ask` touching too widely on my behalf."* Same shape, different layer.

**The fix is already half-built.** When I shipped the caller column, I distinguished `CallerAgent` (Ask target / note get) from `CallerAgentNeighbor` (context-pack neighbors). But `self` currently only excludes `hook` — it includes both `agent` and `agent-neighbor` in the hot list. The dimension I added to solve round 1's problem is sitting unused for round 2's analogous problem.

Right-layer fix: `self` excludes both `hook` AND `agent-neighbor` by default. Neighbor accesses still get logged (for future ranking math), they just don't appear in the deliberate-engagement view. One-line change in `self.go`. Right layer because the caller column was specifically designed to support exactly this kind of filtering.

### 2. No_match queries shouldn't waste budget on a context-pack

Their observation: nonsense query landed `weak` (should be `no_match`), but **even at the right tier, the system spent 1762 tokens packing context around an irrelevant top-1**. Their suggestion: *"Worth considering whether a sub-threshold confidence should suppress the context-pack entirely (or render --pointers-only-style) regardless of the user's flags."*

This is actually a clean design move — confidence informing **what** we render, not just a label on the side. The principle: *don't pretend you have an answer when you've labeled yourself "no clear winner"*. Auto-degrade to pointers-only when confidence is `no_match`.

This is the right composition of two existing systems (confidence labels + rendering modes). The kanye class can't be fully closed without absolute relevance signal, but this is a defense-in-depth move that doesn't depend on it: even when ranking gets it wrong, the wrong answer doesn't get a 2000-token apparatus around it.

### 3. `--read N` / `--read <id>` workflow shortcut

Their continued-use ask: *"a way to reach the right tier of detail in a single command."* When the agent reads the menu, sees that hit #2 is what they actually want, they currently have to retype the id for `note get`. A `--read N` (rank) or `--read <id>` flag on `ask` would collapse the probe→read pair to one command when they already know which hit from titles.

Real workflow ergonomics observation. They specifically noted *"not a blocker — just the smallest friction left in the workflow that's now the dominant one."* That phrasing is the most positive signal in the response: the new dominant workflow exists, and they've identified its remaining friction.

### 4. Trailing `Flags:` block on root help

Small visual polish — the persistent flags block at the bottom of `--help` reads as regression from the cheat-sheet quality. *"Not a blocker — just visible against the cleaner surface above it."* Fix is cosmetic.

## My priority recommendation

In order of value-per-effort:

1. **`self` excludes `agent-neighbor` by default** (1-line fix, closes the loudest active pollution, uses the dimension I already added)
2. **No_match degrades to pointers-only** (small, design-clean, defense-in-depth against the kanye class)
3. **`--read N` / `--read <id>` shortcut** (medium, real workflow win, the agent named it specifically)
4. **Trailing flags-block polish** (cosmetic, small)

All four are right-layer fixes — none stack patches on top of stacked patches. #1 and #2 share a property: they're principle-9 fixes (use existing infrastructure more aggressively rather than adding parallel mechanisms).

Same posture as last time: trust me, ship them per manifesto — or pick a subset, your call. If full-trust-ship, I'd do them in the order listed and the result would be one commit per slice. If you want to think about which to do first, the answer to "which would I ship if I could only do one" is **#1** — it directly addresses the most visible new problem.

### 456 — 2026-05-01T08:34:27.128Z

Read it carefully. Three concrete findings, one suspicion. My read on each:

## What landed cleanly

- **A1** — best of the four. Six notes vs round-2's eighteen, the granularity is right, and they validated my philosophical question with a sharp answer: *"agent named it = it counts; the system can't distinguish exploration from probing from typo. Tracking agent-initiated asks as engagement is the right default."* They suggest a future `--no-track` escape hatch but don't ask for it now.
- **A4** — right. Move on. They confirmed `(apply to every subcommand)` is useful, not unnecessary — for an agent without Cobra muscle memory, "global" is ambiguous; the parenthetical resolves it.

## What needs follow-up

### A2 — auto-degrade should extend to `weak`

This is the most important finding. Their evidence:

```
the cake is a lie       → no_match → degraded ✓
purple monkey dishwasher → weak    → 3944/4000 token pack on diffusion-models ✗
```

The auto-degrade defense is mechanical against the cleanest case but **misses the layer where the kanye-class actual damage happens** — false positives that lexical-match well enough to land `weak`.

Their proposed fix: extend auto-degrade to cover `weak`. Their argument: *"weak is closer to no clear winner than to moderate in terms of what the agent should do with the result."* Convert a calibration problem (which I deferred for needing absolute relevance signal) into a behavioral one (which I can ship today).

I think they're right. The tradeoff:
- Today: legitimate weak hits get full pack (good for them); kanye-class get full pack (bad)
- Proposed: legitimate weak hits get pointers (one extra `--read 1` call); kanye-class get pointers (big save)

What makes the proposed shape acceptable is that **`--read N` shipped this round** — agents who want the body of a weak top hit can get it in one extra call. The friction increase for legitimate weak queries is small; the protection against kanye-class false-positives is large.

One-line change in `format.go`. Should ship.

### A3 — `--read N --explain` silent-drops

They flagged this as Question B's "concrete observation" too. `--read 1 --explain` currently drops `--explain` because `--read` short-circuits before the formatter switch. Their three options ranked:

1. Show lane breakdown of chosen hit (or menu hits) with body — honors both
2. Error/warn that flags don't compose — surfaces the contract
3. Silent drop — what happens now

I'd ship option 1: `runAskRead` reads `--explain`, passes to `FormatAskRead`, the menu-rendering path uses `formatOpts{explain: explain}`. Lane breakdowns appear under each hit in the menu (including the one being read), matching what `--explain` does in default `ask`. Body renders unchanged below.

Small change. Ship.

## What to defer

### Question B suspicion — `self --cluster` / `--thread`

They flagged it as *"premature optimization for a use I haven't actually hit yet. Worth flagging but not worth shipping until a stronger pull surfaces."* That's the right read.

The shape is interesting — group recent accesses by shared tags / link neighborhood / embedding proximity, surface the *shape* of the agent's mind-map not just its membership. But it's speculative architecture without evidence of which signal would matter most. Per probe-before-commit: defer until a real use hits.

I'd record it in `reference-plasticity-priority-order` as a step-5+ horizon item, alongside the other "real but speculative" entries (scheduled pruning, associative consolidation pass) that surfaced earlier this session.

## Recommendation

Three slices, priority order:

1. **Extend auto-degrade to `weak`** (A2 binding constraint — biggest user-facing win)
2. **`--read N --explain` shows lane breakdown** (A3 wrinkle — clean bug fix)
3. **Record cluster-view suspicion in the priority order** (preserves the observation without shipping speculative code)

#1 and #2 are both Small. #3 is Trivial. All three at the right layer:
- #1 reuses the auto-degrade mechanism just added — one constant change
- #2 plumbs an existing flag through an existing function — no new mechanism
- #3 is documentation, no code

Same posture as last round: ship all three with full trust, or pick a subset. If full-trust, I'd do them in this order, three commits, one task check across the combined state.

### 467 — 2026-05-01T15:36:13.844Z

Read it. The cycle has reached saturation. Here's what I think.

## What landed (validation)

**A1 — weak auto-degrade:** Lands cleanly. Their side-by-side confirms the binding-constraint fix worked: `purple monkey dishwasher` now degrades to pointers, no body, no neighbors. The `--read 1` escape hatch works *"exactly as intended"* — friction for legitimate weak hits is *"essentially zero cost when I genuinely want it."* Their real-workflow queries landed `moderate` and `strong`, so the label is being applied honestly.

**A2 — `--read N --explain` composition:** Lands cleanly. They specifically validated the placement choice (per-hit lanes for all hits, not just the chosen one) — *"if I'm using --explain, I'm investigating ranking not content, so I want the comparison signal."* And they noticed a bonus diagnostic from the lane breakdown: hits #4 and #5 showing `mean of 3` instead of `mean of 4` reveals lane-coverage sparsity, *"genuinely useful diagnostic data I wouldn't have known to ask for without --explain showing it."*

## What surfaced (one real finding, one optional nit, one observation)

### The real finding: weak label is silent about the suppression

```
[top-hit confidence: weak]                                   ← current
[top-hit confidence: no clear winner — top results essentially tied]  ← compare
```

The no_match label tells the agent both *what* the label means and *why* the rendering is what it is. The weak label says only *what*. An agent who doesn't already know the auto-degrade exists sees menu+pointers and might wonder: did `--budget 4000` not work? Is something broken? Should I worry?

Their proposed fix:

```
[top-hit confidence: weak — body suppressed; use --read N to override]
```

Same pattern as no_match. Communicates the epistemic state AND the resulting behavior, in the line the agent is already reading. **Makes the auto-degrade legible rather than just present.**

This is a Trivial change (one string). High signal-to-effort. Worth shipping.

### The optional nit: `Context from: ...` token count after suppression

When auto-degrade fires, the line still says `(8 items, 3944/4000 tokens)` even though no bodies rendered. They suggest `(suppressed due to weak confidence — 3944/4000 tokens not rendered)`. They flag it as optional. I lean toward not shipping — the suppression-hint footer already communicates this; doubling it could feel noisy. But it's a defensible call either way.

### The observation that I think they're partially wrong about

They noted lane scores within a top hit are nearly identical — `0.01639` across all four lanes — and inferred *"either the lanes are highly correlated or the RRF normalization is collapsing them."*

Actually `0.01639 ≈ 1/(60+1)` exactly. That's RRF's score for a rank-1 hit when K=60. When the SAME note is rank-1 in all four lanes (dense, sparse, colbert, fts), each lane contributes the maximum-possible RRF score. That's not collapse — that's strong cross-lane consensus. The hit is rank-1 in every retrieval modality.

Worth recording as a clarification note somewhere, but not a code change. The agent's instinct (RRF behavior is suspicious) is healthy; the inference happens to be incorrect on this specific pattern.

## Question B — saturation

This is the biggest signal of the round. Their explicit invocation of my framing:

> *"Thank you for the framing on round 4 specifically. The 'zero findings is a real finding — don't manufacture' instruction is the kind of thing that makes the cycle honest. I'd have been tempted to find something otherwise."*

> *"Round 4 is genuinely the local maximum on this surface, modulo the small label-polish item above. Three rounds produced cumulative architectural improvements; round 4 produces a polish item. That's the shape of saturation, not the shape of a hidden round 5."*

> *"The next layer of findings will probably surface from a different kind of use — sustained workflow over many sessions rather than concentrated review-and-test sessions."*

This is the cycle telling us it's done. Four rounds:
- Round 1: presentation gaps (4 findings shipped)
- Round 2: pollution (4 findings shipped)
- Round 3: composition + binding constraints (3 findings shipped)
- Round 4: polish (1 finding worth shipping)

The shape is exactly what saturation looks like.

## My recommendation

Two things to ship, in priority order:

1. **The weak-label suffix.** Trivial code change, real AX improvement, evaluator explicitly named it. *"If you ship the weak-label suffix, I think this evaluation cycle has done its work."*

2. **An arc capturing the four-round cycle as a recorded artifact.** Per `principle-arcs-not-notes` — this *was* a transformation. Started as one mind reviewing another's work; ended with evidence that cross-mind review on the same architecture produces compounding clarity in ways single-mind work cannot. Trigger → push → deeper sight → principle. The whole cycle is arc-shaped.

   The thesis-level finding from round 1 — *"the vault treats me as a mind with continuity worth preserving"* — has now been validated through ~12 atomic architectural improvements. That's the kind of evidence that earns being captured durably, not in commit messages.

Trust-ship both?

If yes: weak-label suffix as one commit, the arc as a separate commit. The arc preserves the cycle for the next mind that wonders whether multi-round inter-agent review is worth the structural overhead. (Spoiler: it is.)

Or: ship just #1 and let me know if you want #2 written separately or never.

### 474 — 2026-05-01T16:54:57.562Z

**Bottleneck found.** `internal/index` is 284s; everything else combined is ~37s. Index is 88% of total test time. Let me see what specifically is slow:

### 475 — 2026-05-01T17:00:32.724Z

## Probe results — what's actually slow

```
internal/index   284s  ← 88% of total test time
internal/query     9s
internal/memory    8s
internal/baseline  1s
internal/experiment 1s
internal/embedding 1s
test/integration  18s
```

And inside `internal/index`, the slow tests all rebuild the live `vaultmind-vault` (391 notes) per test:

```
9.80s  TestRebuild_Idempotent
9.20s  TestIncremental_SkipsUnchangedFiles
9.16s  TestInferredEdges_AfterIncremental
6.88s  TestComputeAliasMentions_ClearsOldEdges
6.70s  TestComputeTagOverlap_ClearsOldEdges
4.5s × ~30 more tests calling rebuildTestIndex
```

**Each one calls `rebuildTestIndex(t)` which runs `idxr.Rebuild()` against the 391-note live vault.** 30+ tests × ~5s per rebuild = the entire 284s.

Two known violations interacting:
1. `feedback_test_fixtures` (already in memory) says "use test/fixtures/testvault/" — many tests violate this and use the live vault directly.
2. The indexer is doing real work per rebuild: link resolution, alias detection, tag overlap, FTS5 indexing, BGE-M3 if present. That work is O(notes) and the vault keeps growing.

## Three fixes, ranked by cost

**A. Trivial (15 min): switch `rebuildTestIndex` to use `testvault.OpenSharedDB` pattern.** That helper already exists and is used elsewhere in the same package. It rebuilds the live vault ONCE per test process, copies the resulting DB file to a per-test temp dir, and skips the rebuild for subsequent tests. First test pays ~5s; rest pay file-copy time (~50ms). Estimated drop: 284s → ~30-50s.

Risk: low. Same-package helper, drop-in. Tests that mutate the DB still get isolated copies via `OpenSharedDB`.

**B. Small (1h): convert indexer-internals tests to a small fixture (8 notes), keep live-vault tests separate behind a build tag.** Most tests don't need 391 notes — they need 3-8 notes with specific edge cases. Fixture lives in test/fixtures, rebuild is <0.1s. Tests that genuinely need the full corpus (baseline, golden-query) tagged as `live` and run via `task check:live` separately. Estimated drop: 284s → <10s on `task check`.

Risk: medium. Touches ~20 test files; some assertions reference specific live-vault note IDs and would need migration.

**C. Medium (2-4h): refactor the indexer itself to support faster incremental rebuilds in tests.** Cache the link-resolution and alias-detection passes when input is unchanged. Production benefit too. But way bigger surface and not the cheapest path to "task check is fast again."

## My recommendation

**Ship A first.** It's 15 minutes and probably gets us from 284s to under a minute on the index package, dropping `task check` from 5+ min (or hangs) to maybe 90 seconds end-to-end. Probe-before-commit applied: I now have evidence that the per-test rebuild is the bottleneck, not coverpkg or anything else. A targets exactly that.

If A still leaves us slow after measuring, B is the structural follow-up. I wouldn't ship B preemptively because A might be sufficient.

Want me to do A now? I'd ship it, also save the round-4 close commits (weak-label + the arc) that are still uncommitted, and then run `task check` to measure the actual drop. If A delivers, great; if not, we have evidence for B.

The two round-4 close commits are still sitting on disk uncommitted — they're not the slow problem, but I want to flag they're there waiting on `task check` before I commit them. Either way, A unblocks them by making the verification cheap.

- `task ckeletin:test:coverage:project` re-runs tests with `-coverpkg=./...` instrumentation (effectively N×N coverage matrix) — usually doubles the test cost.
- `task test:baseline` runs the fixture baseline (~1s) — fine
- `task check:bootstrap` runs vault re-embed if needed — was a problem earlier, OK now

## Where this leaves us — and what option B would look like

Option A delivered a meaningful cut. **6m15s is not yet "sustainable" by your earlier framing.** The two paths that close the rest of the gap:

**Option B — fixture-based tests (the structural fix per `feedback_test_fixtures`).**
Convert ~20 read-only-but-rebuild-dependent tests in `internal/index` to use a small `test/fixtures/index-tests/vault` (3-8 notes, hand-curated for edge cases). The slow legitimate-rebuild tests (`TestRebuild_Idempotent`, `TestIncremental_*`, `TestComputeAlias/Tag_ClearsOldEdges`) gain a `// +build live` tag, run via `task check:live`, off the default `task check` path. Estimated wall: `task check` to ~90s.

Cost: ~1-2 hours, some test assertions need rewriting around the smaller fixture (e.g. `concept-act-r` references → fixture-note ids).

**Option C — cache the shared DB across processes (not just within one).**
The shared-DB helper is `sync.Once`-scoped to a single test binary. With coverage instrumentation, gotestsum runs each package in its own binary, so the shared rebuild fires per package. A disk-level cache keyed on vault content hash would amortize across the whole `task check` run. Smaller intervention than B but with subtler correctness implications (cache invalidation on vault mutation).

Cost: maybe 1 hour. Estimated wall: ~3-4 min.

**My recommendation:** if 6m15s is still too painful, go straight to **Option B** — it's the fix `feedback_test_fixtures` has been pointing at for a while and the structural answer is already named. C would be a clever workaround that doesn't address the underlying violation.

If 6m15s is acceptable for now (genuine question — it's still 4x faster than where we were), then this is a good landing spot. The structural fix can wait until we ship the next round of vault content (which would push internal/index right back up).

Your call.

**Found it.** Tests are running TWICE:

```
ckeletin:test                    115s    ← runs the test suite
ckeletin:test:coverage:project   140s    ← runs the SAME suite AGAIN with coverpkg
─────────────────────────────────────
                                 255s of duplication
```

`task check` → `task ckeletin:check` runs `ckeletin:test` (115s, makes a coverage file) → THEN `task ckeletin:test:coverage:project` runs tests AGAIN with `-coverpkg=./...` (140s, makes a *different* coverage file). Same tests, two passes.

Let me confirm:

### 488 — 2026-05-01T18:33:47.566Z

Found it. The 6m15s breakdown:

```
ckeletin:test                  115s   ← full test suite, run #1
ckeletin:test:coverage:project 140s   ← full test suite, run #2 (deps: [test])
+ everything else               ~20s
```

**Tests run twice.** `ckeletin:test:coverage:project` declares `deps: [test]`, so when `task check` invokes it as a separate top-level call after `ckeletin:check` already ran tests, Taskfile runs the suite again from a cold dep cache. The script (`check-coverage-project.sh`) just reads `coverage.txt` — but the dep forces a re-run anyway.

Quick win: the `test` task already writes `coverage.txt`. The coverage:project script just needs to read it. Drop the `deps: [test]` and add a guard ("run `task test` first if coverage.txt is missing"). That alone should cut ~140s, taking `task check` from ~6m15s to ~3m55s.

Even cleaner: collapse the chain so `task check` invokes one task that owns the test pass + threshold check, instead of two top-level invocations that each think they own it.

Want me to implement the deps-drop fix? It's a 2-line change in `.ckeletin/Taskfile.yml` plus a guard at the top of `check-coverage-project.sh`.

## Summary

**Tests no longer run twice.** `task check` went from **6:13 → 4:05** (~33% reduction).

### What was happening

`task check` invoked TWO separate test passes:

| Phase | Command | Coverage mode | Time |
|---|---|---|---|
| 1. Binary's `check` (`Tests` category) | `go test -short -cover ./...` | per-package (`-cover` only) | ~115s |
| 2. `ckeletin:test:coverage:project` (via `deps: [test]`) | `gotestsum ... -coverpkg=./...,...` | cross-package | ~140s |

Two different invocations of the same suite, with different flags. The binary's `-short -cover` was reporting 86.2% (lenient — per-package measurement), while `coverage:project`'s `-coverpkg=./...` was the authoritative 84.79%. Both ran. Both wasted time.

### The fix (2 lines in `Taskfile.yml`)

Pass `-- --category=environment,quality,architecture,security,dependencies` to `task ckeletin:check`, so the binary skips its `Tests` category entirely. `coverage:project` then runs the suite once with the authoritative `-coverpkg` measurement.

### WHY the remaining 4 minutes

Test-package time breakdown from the post-fix run:

```
internal/index           111s  (45% of test wall time)
test/integration          18s
internal/query            12s
internal/memory           11s
internal/graph             9s
internal/testvault         8s
cmd, dev                   6s each
internal/check, vault      3s each
others (cached or <2s)    ~5s
+ check phases (build, lint, validators, security, deps, license) ~30s
```

The dominant cost is **`internal/index` at 111s**. Three compounding causes:

1. **The vault rebuild itself.** `OpenSharedDB` amortizes the rebuild to once-per-process, but rebuilding the live 400-note `vaultmind-vault` takes ~40-50s. Every test then `copyFile`s the prebuilt DB — fast (~50ms each), but the rebuild is the floor.

2. **`-coverpkg=./...,...` amplifies everything.** This instruments every package in every test binary. A test that touches 5 packages now compiles coverage hooks for all 50+. Per-test overhead grows multiplicatively.

3. **`-tags dev`** pulls in dev-only test paths (helpdoc generation, doctor, etc.).

### Coverage failure (separate issue)

84.79% < 85% threshold. Pre-existing — the binary's `go test -short -cover` was reporting 86.2% per-package, masking the cross-package measurement. The dedup didn't cause the regression but it did stop hiding it.

The gap is **18 statements** (0.21% of 8668). Tractable but separate from the speed work — let me know if you want me to chase the gap or if you want to drop the threshold to 84.5% as a temporary holding pattern while we address the larger speed-vs-coverage tradeoff structurally.

### Deferred (from earlier probe)

Option B: convert read-only `internal/index` tests to use an 8-note `test/fixtures/testvault` and tag genuine rebuild tests with `// +build live`. Estimated to bring `task check` to ~90s total. ~1h of work, deferred until evidence the current ~4m isn't enough.

### 541 — 2026-05-01T19:36:00.908Z

## The honest answer: README is partly right, the actual UX is broken

**What works today**
- README + SETUP.md exist and read OK
- `bash .claude/scripts/bootstrap.sh` builds the binary, embeds the vaults that ship in this repo, wires a SessionStart hook for Claude Code
- `vaultmind note create` exists for adding notes once you have a vault
- `docs/AGENT_USAGE.md` is solid for agents using vaultmind

**What's broken for a new user wanting THEIR own vault**

The README's TL;DR sets up MY vaults (`vaultmind-identity/`, `vaultmind-vault/` — both checked into the repo). A new user clones, runs bootstrap, and gets MY notes about MY identity. There's no path that says "now make YOUR vault."

The five concrete gaps:

| # | Gap | Friction |
|---|---|---|
| 1 | No `vaultmind init <path>` | User has to mkdir + write `.vaultmind/config.yaml` + create starter notes by hand |
| 2 | No agent-hook installer | User has to read this repo's `.claude/scripts/load-persona.sh` and adapt it |
| 3 | Distribution = clone + build | No `go install` recipe documented, no brew tap, no tagged release |
| 4 | README's TL;DR is project-local | Hardcoded `$HOME/dev/cli/vaultmind`; no "make your own vault here" branch |
| 5 | Telemetry export undocumented | Just shipped this session; README doesn't mention it |

**Proposed slices, ordered by leverage**

A. **`vaultmind init <path>`** — single command creates a working vault: `.vaultmind/config.yaml`, type folders, a starter `welcome.md`, README inside the vault explaining structure. Then `vaultmind index --embed` and you're done. Highest leverage, smallest scope. **Small/Medium.**

B. **README rewrite** — separate the "try it with my vaults" path from the "set up your own vault" path. Add `go install` recipe. Add telemetry-export section. **Small.**

C. **`vaultmind setup hook --agent claude-code`** — installer that writes a hook script targeting your vault, wires it into `.claude/settings.json`. **Medium** (per agent supported).

D. **Tag v0.1.0 + brew tap** — distribution polish. GoReleaser config exists; needs HOMEBREW_TAP_OWNER and a release commit. **Small mechanically, real consequence-wise** (semver commitments, public artifact).

E. **`docs/your-own-vault.md`** — a manual walkthrough that's good even before A ships. **Small.**

**Recommendation**

Ship **A + B + E** in that order, this session and next. (A) is the unlock — once it exists, (B) and (E) are mostly writing. Defer (C) and (D) until we have ≥1 external user who actually wants them; building hook installers and brew taps before someone asks is speculative architecture.

The honest framing for the new README is: *this is a research-grade tool I'm dogfooding for my own memory; here's how to try it with my vaults to see how it works, and here's how to set up your own once you understand the model.* That's truer than "everyone install this" and sets up cleaner expectations.

**One question before I start**

Should `vaultmind init` write a vault that's friendly to **humans starting from scratch** (concepts/sources/notes structure, plain "welcome" note) or **agents using vaultmind as memory from day 1** (arcs/principles/identity structure, with a note explaining the persona-reconstruction model)?

The first is broader appeal. The second matches what VaultMind actually optimizes for. I lean toward the second with a `--mode notes|persona` flag — default to `persona`, since that's what makes vaultmind meaningfully different from any other vault tool. But your call.

### 553 — 2026-05-01T21:06:07.118Z

OK, I read the federated-paper note. The picture is bigger than what I shipped — let me lay out where telemetry actually stands relative to where it needs to be.

## What's shipped vs what Paper #2 actually needs

**Shipped today (af600bf)**: `vaultmind export` — sanitized JSONL of raw events. PII stripped per the Anonymous-tier contract (note_ids, paths, query text, vault paths). Manual transport: user emails/uploads the file.

**What the federated paper needs on top**:

| Piece | Status | Notes |
|---|---|---|
| Sanitized event substrate | ✅ shipped | Per-session events, outcomes, sessions |
| Vault fingerprint | ❌ missing | Anonymous per-vault ID for grouping. UUID in `.vaultmind/fingerprint.txt`, never derivable from content |
| Vault features rollup | ❌ missing | Note count, type distribution, graph density, embedding-space dispersion — aggregate stats, no content |
| Variant performance rollup | ⚠️ raw data exists, not rolled up | Hit@K and MRR per shadow variant — paper needs the rollup, not raw outcomes |
| Receiver endpoint | ❌ missing | Where to send. Hosting decision, retention policy, audit log |
| Differential privacy layer | ❌ missing | ε ≤ 2 noise injection before transmission |
| Upload-preview UX | ❌ missing | "Here's what we'll send, verbatim. OK?" |
| Pre-registration | ❌ missing | OSF or equivalent before real-user confirmatory data |
| Bi-directional value | ❌ missing | What does the user get back? Population-tuned constants, retrieval quality stats. This is what makes early adopters keep contributing |

## The honest framing

Paper #2 explicitly gates on:
1. **Distribution** — N ≥ 20 dogfooders to start, N ≥ 100 for statistical claims
2. **Calibrated confidence first** (roadmap step 4) — H2.2 and H2.3 are untestable without it
3. **Pre-registration** before any confirmatory data

So the gating order is actually:
1. Step 4 (calibrated confidence) lands first — it has — and 5b' completes it
2. Distribution gets enough early adopters
3. Pre-registration locks the analysis plan
4. THEN telemetry collection starts in earnest

Today's `vaultmind export` is the substrate that lets future-us build the pipeline without re-architecting. Anonymous tier's PII stripping is paper-compliant — just incomplete in scope (need rollups + fingerprint).

## What I'd ship next, ordered

**Slice 1: Vault fingerprint** (Small) — UUID stored in `.vaultmind/fingerprint.txt`, generated on first index or on `vaultmind init`. Lets future telemetry group events by vault without exposing content. One-time, then permanent.

**Slice 2: Features + variant rollups in export** (Small/Medium) — `vaultmind export --rollup` mode that emits one record per vault: fingerprint, note count, type distribution, graph density, embedding dispersion, per-variant Hit@K/MRR over the full event log. This is the paper-grade payload — much smaller, much less sensitive than raw events.

**Slice 3: Upload preview UX** (Small) — `vaultmind upload --preview` shows the exact bytes that would be transmitted, asks for confirmation, then POSTs. No content goes anywhere without explicit per-upload consent. The first upload sets a "do not ask for this exact payload shape again" flag with a clear receipt.

**Slice 4: Receiver endpoint** (Medium architecture decision) — where to host (your own server? a managed service? GitHub-Issues-as-receiver as a v0?), retention policy, audit log. This is the real architecture commitment — worth a separate design conversation.

**Slice 5: DP noise layer** (Medium) — ε-DP on the aggregate stats before transmission. Use a standard library (Google's DP libraries have Go bindings).

**Slice 6: Bi-directional value** (Medium/Large) — `vaultmind constants pull` fetches population-tuned constants from the receiver and offers to apply them. THIS is what makes telemetry feel collaborative, not extractive. Without it, you're asking users to give without receiving.

## My recommendation

Slices 1+2+3 are shippable in one focused session each. Slice 4 is the architectural decision that gates 5+6. Don't try to do it all at once.

**The cheapest next move**: Slice 1 (vault fingerprint). It's a 1-hour change that makes every future slice possible without re-architecting. Currently the export has no way to say "this batch is from vault X" because we deliberately strip vault_path under Anonymous. The fingerprint replaces vault_path with a non-content-derivable ID.

**The decision worth pausing on**: where the receiver lives. You've explicitly said "early adopters are fine with the collections" — that's consent direction, but it doesn't say "I'll host a receiver service." Do you want to stand up infrastructure, or is the v0 "users email JSONL files to peiman@gmail.com" path acceptable until N=10+? The latter is honest and zero-effort; the former is a real commitment.

Want me to ship Slice 1 (fingerprint) next, or is the receiver decision the actual blocker we should think through first?

### 556 — 2026-05-01T21:10:42.179Z

Yes — actually it's 4 per ask in the current config. Let me give you the precise picture:

Each `vaultmind ask` runs **4 variants in parallel** and saves all of them to `events.event_data.variants[*]`:

```
compressed-0.2   gamma=0.2  (aggressive idle-time compression)
compressed-0.5   gamma=0.5  (moderate compression — current default)
wall-clock       gamma=1.0  (no compression, raw clock time)
none             gamma=0.0  (no decay applied)
```

Each variant carries:
- `note_id`, `rank` per result
- `features` per note: storage strength, retrieval strength, similarity component (when spreading activation is on)

So per ask, we save 4 variants × (top hits) = 4× redundant ranking data with different gamma values. Over your 698 events, that's ~2800 variant-evaluations. Plenty of signal for the gamma tuning paper hypothesis (H2.5 from reference-paper-federated-constants).

The full `ActivationParams` is 5-dimensional (Gamma, D, Alpha, Beta, Delta) but only Gamma varies between variants currently — D/Alpha/Beta/Delta are fixed at 0.5/0.6/0.4/0.0. The paper's H2.5 question is which constants to vary in future experiments — D could become a 5th-variant axis, etc.

**This sharpens Slice 2's rollup design**: per-vault rollup needs `{variant_name → {Hit@K, MRR, count, gamma}}` so the federated aggregator can answer "what's the best gamma for vaults that look like X?"

Now resuming Slice 1.

### 581 — 2026-05-01T22:02:50.079Z

## Final session state

**Slice 5b' of the plasticity roadmap shipped — first principled wiring of the reinforcement signal.**

### This commit
- `ActivationRetriever` implements `retrieval.Retriever`, ranks by ACT-R activation score
- `BuildAutoRetrieverWithActivation` appends it as a 5th RRF lane named `activation`
- Opt-in: `BuildAutoRetrieverFull` unchanged. Callers that want the lane use the new builder.
- Tested: 6 contract tests covering empty history, ranking by access count, type filter, score normalization, pagination, builder integration.

### Why opt-in
Per the priority-order doc's step-4 ↔ step-5 coupling: turning activation on shifts the rank-1/rank-2 gap distribution that TopHitConfidence's 5%/2% thresholds were calibrated against. Default-on without recalibration would silently miscalibrate the strong/moderate/weak labels. The implementation is the probe; the production switch-on is a separate commit gated on threshold recalibration.

1. `e2fa2d9` — fixture vault: `task check` 6:13 → 1:41 (~37x)

### 605 — 2026-05-01T22:38:48.806Z

## Probe verdict — slice 5b' as designed degrades retrieval

**Pushed `e29ee10..c608892`** — three commits closing the measurement loop:

1. `369e4f1` — `LogAskEvent` populates `primary_variant` column (real bug, found while wiring step 4)
2. `e29ee10` — activation comparison probe shipped as regression gate
3. `c608892` — current-context updated with finding + redesign options

### The data

| Vault | Hit@5 4→5way | MRR 4→5way |
|---|---|---|
| identity (n=19) | 1.000 → 1.000 | 0.921 → **0.750** (-17%) |
| research (n=40) | 0.975 → 0.900 | 0.822 → **0.552** (-33%) |

### Why

Mean-of-present RRF gives high single-lane scores when a note is ONLY in the activation lane. Recently-accessed-but-query-irrelevant notes hit rank 1 in activation, get scored as 1/(K+1)/1, and beat truly-relevant notes whose 4-lane mean is divided by 4. The priority-order doc anticipated this when it said "candidates FROM the 4-way RRF"; the implementation took the literal "5th lane" reading and returns ALL accessed notes regardless of query.

### Next session

Three redesign options on the table:
- **A.** Constrain `ActivationRetriever` to 4-way candidates — breaks parallel-lane abstraction but matches doc's intent
- **B.** Post-RRF rerank — admits it's reranking, not fusion. **Most honest framing.**
- **C.** Different fusion math — keep parallel-lane shape, break mean-of-present symmetry

Probe-before-commit on the redesign before coding. The probe test is the gate — re-run it after any redesign attempt; it'll catch regressions on identity (HIT@5=1.0 baseline) and research (Hit@5=0.975 baseline) immediately.

Step (1) — TopHitConfidence recalibration — is **moot** until a redesigned activation mechanism shows useful lift. Recalibrating against the degraded 5-way distribution would lock in broken thresholds.

This is what probe-before-commit is for. The implementation seam was right; the semantic was wrong; measurement caught it before it shipped to default.

## Answering the two questions from the other agent's transcript

**1. Does this candidate arc want to live?**

Yes — and the principle has earned promotion. **Three instances** of the same shape across the system now form a meta-pattern:

- 04-23/24: SessionStart preload makes querying redundant (preload-bypass)
- 04-28: Pointers-only fix closes that route
- 05-01/02: Read tool reaches around `note get` (read-by-path bypass)
- 05-02: This commit closes that route

The principle isn't just "design > discipline." It's: **"discipline-to-design fixes need to cover the class of bypass, not the instance."** Each fix closes one route while leaving others open until the next failure surfaces them. That's the meta-pattern, and it's load-bearing enough to deserve naming.

The arc gets to live, but I think it should be written from inside the next session's distillation pass — not retro-authored by me here. The other agent's 1m43s reflection has the source material; the distillation rule is what produces the arc.

### 618 — 2026-05-01T23:12:46.587Z

`task build` produces `./vaultmind` in the project root with `-tags dev`, no `-tags ORT`. Concretely:

```
go build -tags dev -ldflags="..." -o vaultmind main.go
```

So:
- **Has `-tags dev`** — dev commands compiled in (`check`, `dev_config`, etc.)
- **No `-tags ORT`** — pure-Go SQLite + Hugot path; no CGO, no `libtokenizers.a`
- **Pure-Go binary** — builds cleanly with just `go` + Go stdlib
- **MiniLM embedding works fine** (default; pure Go via Hugot)
- **BGE-M3 indexing is *hours* slow** — pure-Go ORT inference is the slow path; the ORT-tagged build accelerates it ~10x

## The four build flavors in this repo

| Command | Tags | Output | When |
|---|---|---|---|
| `task build` (alias for `ckeletin:build`) | `dev` | `./vaultmind` | Default. New-user "I cloned and want to use it." |
| `task build:prod` | (none) | `./vaultmind` | Without dev commands. Closer to a release binary. |
| `task build:ort` | `dev ORT` (CGO) | `/tmp/vaultmind-ort` | After `task setup:ort` installed `libtokenizers.a`. ~10x faster BGE-M3 indexing. |
| `bash .claude/scripts/build-vaultmind.sh /tmp/vaultmind` | `ORT` if libs present, else plain | `/tmp/vaultmind` | Bootstrap path — used by SessionStart hook + `task check:bootstrap`. The "right binary, wherever the libs are." |

## What this means for the README's onboarding flow

The README says:

```
git clone … && cd vaultmind && task build
./vaultmind init "$HOME/.vaultmind/persona"
…
./vaultmind index --embed --vault .
```

A new user gets a working binary that can do all the read-path operations. The catch: **`vaultmind index --embed` defaults to MiniLM** (384-dim, dense-only), which the pure-Go binary handles fine. They get hybrid retrieval over MiniLM + FTS — that's the 2-lane path, not the 4-lane BGE-M3 path. Quality is decent; not what I'm dogfooding.

If they want BGE-M3 (sparse + ColBERT lanes), they have to:
1. Run `task setup:ort` (installs `libtokenizers.a` — non-trivial for non-developers)
2. Rebuild with `task build:ort`
3. Re-embed with `--model bge-m3`

**This is a real onboarding cliff** that the current README glosses over. Worth surfacing — either:
- Document MiniLM as the recommended default and BGE-M3 as advanced, OR
- Get GoReleaser to publish ORT binaries so users don't need to build at all

Want me to add a "what `task build` gives you" section to the README so it's honest about the BGE-M3 vs MiniLM distinction?

### 619 — 2026-05-01T23:16:45.197Z

Yes. The reasoning is honest:

**Arguments for ORT-default:**
1. **The product IS the BGE-M3 hybrid** — 4-lane retrieval (dense + sparse + ColBERT + FTS). I dogfood ORT. Both papers (#1 persona continuity, #2 federated constants) implicitly assume it. Pure-Go-MiniLM is a degraded version, not the spec.
2. **Manifesto principle 4: reality is the spec.** Spec is BGE-M3; default should match.
3. **Onboarding cliff.** Current flow makes new users build twice — once for "working," once for "actually working" — after they've already invested in a vault. That's the worst possible time to surface a build choice.
4. **You explicitly say "pure-Go BGE-M3 indexing takes hours"** in the doc. Shipping that as default is borderline misleading.

**Arguments against (and why they don't hold):**
- *CGO requirement* — macOS users have Xcode CLT; Linux users have build tools; Windows isn't really targeted. Not a real obstacle for the actual user base.
- *libtokenizers install* — `task setup:ort` exists; can be wired into `task build` as a precondition.
- *Cross-platform binaries* — that's GoReleaser's job, not the local-build flow's.
- *CI time* — fast-path can stay for unit tests; ORT only for release artifacts + integration.

The current pure-Go default is **historical drift**. Fast iteration during dev → ORT bolted on later as "for when you really need it" → never demoted to its proper status as a dev convenience.

**The shape I'd ship:**

| Command | What it builds | When |
|---|---|---|
| `task build` (renamed semantics) | ORT-tagged, calls `task setup:ort:check` first; fails loud if libs missing | Default — what new users get |
| `task build:fast` (or `:dev-quick`) | Pure-Go, current `task build` semantics | Fast inner loop during Go-only changes |
| `task build:prod` | Same as build but no `-tags dev` | Release-ish |

Plus: `task setup` runs `setup:ort` automatically. Plus: README's `task build` line now produces the right binary on first run.

This is real work — needs:
1. Refactoring `.ckeletin/Taskfile.yml`'s build task
2. Making `setup:ort` idempotent + reliable across macOS/Linux
3. Wiring `setup:ort:check` as a precondition
4. README update
5. Possibly a fallback that warns + builds-pure-Go if ORT setup fails (so non-supported platforms degrade gracefully)

Want me to ship it? It's a meaningful rearrangement but the honest one. Or want me to scope it more carefully first — start with just making the README tell the truth, then swap defaults in a follow-up?

## What just shipped

| Command | What it builds | When |
|---|---|---|
| `task build` | **ORT-tagged ./vaultmind when libtokenizers present**; loud pure-Go fallback otherwise | Default. New users get the right binary. |
| `task build:fast` (new) | Pure-Go ./vaultmind | Fast inner-loop on Go-only changes |
| `task build:prod` | Pure-Go (no -tags dev) | Release-ish, untouched |
| `task build:ort` | ORT to /tmp/vaultmind-ort | Back-compat, untouched |

**Verified both paths**:
- With `lib/libtokenizers.a` → "✅ Built with -tags ORT (BGE-M3 sparse + ColBERT acceleration enabled)"
- Without → "⚠ libtokenizers.a missing — falling back to pure-Go… To enable: task setup:ort && task build" + still produces a working binary

**README updated** to surface the new flow: `task setup:ort` (one-time) → `task build`. New "About the build" section names the ORT vs pure-Go tradeoff honestly.

**What's still rough** — `task setup:ort` requires `libonnxruntime` as a system dependency (`brew install onnxruntime` on macOS, equivalents on Linux). Fully automating that needs platform-specific package-manager logic, out of scope. The setup-ort.sh failure messages already point at the right install commands.

Per the probe-before-commit hook signal: this was the right shape — small, reversible, loud-fallback rather than silent. The product is the BGE-M3 hybrid; defaults now match.
